<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Happiness</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Mental or emotional state of well-being characterized by pleasant emotions</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Several terms  redirect here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Happiness_(disambiguation)" title="Happiness (disambiguation)">Happiness (disambiguation)</a>, <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Happy_(disambiguation)" title="Happy (disambiguation)">Happy (disambiguation)</a>, <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Gladness_(disambiguation)" title="Gladness (disambiguation)">Gladness (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Jolly_(disambiguation)" title="Jolly (disambiguation)">Jolly (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Enjoyment" redirects here. For the 2005 video album by Kaiser Chiefs, see <a href="/wiki/Enjoyment_(video)" title="Enjoyment (video)">Enjoyment (video)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Cheerful" redirects here. For Royal Navy destroyer, see <a href="/wiki/HMS_Cheerful_(1897)" title="HMS Cheerful (1897)">HMS Cheerful (1897)</a>.</div>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Emotion_sidebar" title="Template:Emotion sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Emotion_sidebar" title="Template talk:Emotion sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Emotion_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Happiness</b>, in the context of <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental</a> or <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">emotional</a> states, is positive or <a href="/wiki/Pleasure" title="Pleasure">pleasant</a> emotions ranging from <a href="/wiki/Contentment" title="Contentment">contentment</a> to intense <a href="/wiki/Joy" title="Joy">joy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-1">[1]</a></sup> Other forms include <a href="/wiki/Life_satisfaction" title="Life satisfaction">life satisfaction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Well-being" title="Well-being">well-being</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjective_well-being" title="Subjective well-being">subjective well-being</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flourishing" title="Flourishing">flourishing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">eudaimonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the 1960s, happiness research has been conducted in a wide variety of scientific disciplines, including <a href="/wiki/Gerontology" title="Gerontology">gerontology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_psychology" title="Social psychology">social psychology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Positive_psychology" title="Positive psychology">positive psychology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clinical_research" title="Clinical research">clinical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medical_research" title="Medical research">medical research</a> and <a href="/wiki/Happiness_economics" title="Happiness economics">happiness economics</a>.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span></h2>
<p>"Happiness" is subject to debate on usage and meaning,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and on possible differences in understanding by culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Veenhoven_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Veenhoven-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Happy_children_splashing_water.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3337" data-file-width="5000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Happy_children_splashing_water.jpg/220px-Happy_children_splashing_water.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Happy_children_splashing_water.jpg/330px-Happy_children_splashing_water.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Happy_children_splashing_water.jpg/440px-Happy_children_splashing_water.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Happy_children_splashing_water.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Happy children playing in water</div></div></div>
<p>The word is mostly used in relation to two factors:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>the current experience of the <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">feeling</a> of an <a href="/wiki/Affect_(psychology)" title="Affect (psychology)">emotion (affect)</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Pleasure" title="Pleasure">pleasure</a> or <a href="/wiki/Joy" title="Joy">joy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-1">[1]</a></sup> or of a more general sense of 'emotional condition as a whole'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> For instance <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Kahneman" title="Daniel Kahneman">Daniel Kahneman</a> has defined happiness as "<i>what I experience here and now</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> This usage is prevalent in dictionary definitions of happiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></li>
<li>appraisal of <a href="/wiki/Life_satisfaction" title="Life satisfaction">life satisfaction</a>, such as of <a href="/wiki/Quality_of_life" title="Quality of life">quality of life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> For instance <a href="/wiki/Ruut_Veenhoven" title="Ruut Veenhoven">Ruut Veenhoven</a> has defined happiness as "overall appreciation of one's life as-a-whole."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Veenhoven_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Veenhoven-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2">:â2â</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Kahneman has said that this is more important to people than current experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Some usages can include both of these factors. <a href="/wiki/Subjective_well-being" title="Subjective well-being">Subjective well-being</a> (swb)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> includes measures of current experience (emotions, <a href="/wiki/Mood_(psychology)" title="Mood (psychology)">moods</a>, and feelings) and of <a href="/wiki/Life_satisfaction" title="Life satisfaction">life satisfaction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[nb 1]</a></sup> For instance <a href="/wiki/Sonja_Lyubomirsky" title="Sonja Lyubomirsky">Sonja Lyubomirsky</a> has described happiness as "<i>the experience of joy, contentment, or positive well-being, combined with a sense that one's life is good, meaningful, and worthwhile.</i>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">Eudaimonia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> is a Greek term variously translated as happiness, welfare, <a href="/wiki/Flourishing" title="Flourishing">flourishing</a>, and blessedness. Xavier Landes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> has proposed that happiness include measures of subjective wellbeing, <a href="/wiki/Mood_(psychology)" title="Mood (psychology)">mood</a> and eudaimonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>These differing uses can give different results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> For instance the correlation of income levels has been shown to be substantial with <a href="/wiki/Life_satisfaction" title="Life satisfaction">life satisfaction</a> measures, but to be far weaker, at least above a certain threshold, with current experience measures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> Whereas Nordic countries often score highest on <a href="/wiki/World_Happiness_Report#2019_World_Happiness_Report" title="World Happiness Report">swb surveys</a>, South American countries score higher on affect-based surveys of current positive life experiencing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The implied meaning of the word may vary depending on context,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> qualifying happiness as a <a href="/wiki/Polysemy" title="Polysemy">polyseme</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Fuzzy_concept" title="Fuzzy concept">fuzzy concept</a>.
</p><p>A further issue is when measurement is made; appraisal of a level of happiness at the time of the experience may be different from appraisal via memory at a later date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some users accept these issues, but continue to use the word because of its convening power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement">Measurement</span></h2>
<p>People have been trying to measure happiness for centuries. In 1780, the English utilitarian philosopher Jeremy Bentham proposed that as happiness was the primary goal of humans it should be measured as a way of determining how well the government was performing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Happiness can be examined in experiential and evaluative contexts. Experiential well-being, or "objective happiness", is happiness measured in the moment via questions such as "How good or bad is your experience now?". In contrast, evaluative well-being asks questions such as "How good was your vacation?" and measures one's subjective thoughts and feelings about happiness in the past. Experiential well-being is less prone to errors in <a href="/wiki/Reconstructive_memory" title="Reconstructive memory">reconstructive memory</a>, but the majority of literature on happiness refers to evaluative well-being. The two measures of happiness can be related by heuristics such as the <a href="/wiki/Peak%E2%80%93end_rule" title="Peakâend rule">peakâend rule</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several scales have been developed to measure happiness:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Subjective_Happiness_Scale&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Subjective Happiness Scale (page does not exist)">Subjective Happiness Scale</a> (SHS) is a four-item scale, measuring global subjective happiness from 1999. The scale requires participants to use absolute ratings to characterize themselves as happy or unhappy individuals, as well as it asks to what extent they identify themselves with descriptions of happy and unhappy individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Affect_measures#PANAS" title="Affect measures">Positive and Negative Affect Schedule</a> (PANAS) from 1988 is a 20-item questionnaire, using a five-point Likert scale (1 = very slightly or not at all, 5 = extremely) to assess the relation between personality traits and positive or negative affects at "this moment, today, the past few days, the past week, the past few weeks, the past year, and in general".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> A longer version with additional affect scales was published 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Satisfaction_with_Life_Scale&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Satisfaction with Life Scale (page does not exist)">Satisfaction with Life Scale</a> (SWLS) is a global cognitive assessment of <a href="/wiki/Life_satisfaction" title="Life satisfaction">life satisfaction</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/Ed_Diener" title="Ed Diener">Ed Diener</a>. A seven-point Likert scale is used to agree or disagree with five statements about one's life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Cantril ladder method<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1047_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1047-44">[43]</a></sup> has been used in the <a href="/wiki/World_Happiness_Report" title="World Happiness Report">World Happiness Report</a>. Respondents are asked to think of a ladder, with the best possible life for them being a 10, and the worst possible life being a 0. They are then asked to rate their own current lives on that 0 to 10 scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1047_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1047-44">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li>Positive Experience; the survey by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallup_(company)" title="Gallup (company)">Gallup</a> asks if, the day before, people experienced enjoyment, laughing or smiling a lot, feeling well-rested, being treated with respect, learning or doing something interesting. 9 of the top 10 countries in 2018 were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_American" title="South American">South American</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>. Country scores range from 85 to 43.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Six-factor_Model_of_Psychological_Well-being" title="Six-factor Model of Psychological Well-being">Ryff scale</a> uses six dimensions to define well-being, they are autonomy, environmental mastery, personal growth, positive relations, purpose in life and self-acceptance. 20 questions are comprised for each aspect of well-being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Since 2012, a <a href="/wiki/World_Happiness_Report" title="World Happiness Report">World Happiness Report</a> has been published. Happiness is evaluated, as in "How happy are you with your life as a whole?", and in emotional reports, as in "How happy are you now?," and people seem able to use happiness as appropriate in these verbal contexts. Using these measures, the report identifies the countries with the highest levels of happiness. In subjective well-being measures, the primary distinction is between cognitive life evaluations and emotional reports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>The UK began to measure national well-being in 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> following <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>, which had already been measuring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gross_national_happiness" title="Gross national happiness">gross national happiness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Academic economists and international economic organizations are arguing for and developing multi-dimensional dashboards which combine subjective and objective indicators to provide a more direct and explicit assessment of human wellbeing. There are many different contributors to adult wellbeing, that happiness judgements partly reflect the presence of salient constraints, and fairness, autonomy, community and engagement are key aspects of happiness and wellbeing throughout the life course.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> Although these factors play a role in happiness, they do not all need to improve simultaneously to help one achieve an increase in happiness. 
</p><p>Happiness has been found to be quite stable over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_happy_butcher.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1807" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/The_happy_butcher.jpg/220px-The_happy_butcher.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/The_happy_butcher.jpg/330px-The_happy_butcher.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/The_happy_butcher.jpg/440px-The_happy_butcher.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_happy_butcher.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A butcher happily slicing meat</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_happiness" title="Philosophy of happiness">Philosophy of happiness</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_morality">Relation to morality</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_happiness" title="Philosophy of happiness">Philosophy of happiness</a> is often discussed in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> Traditional European societies, inherited from the Greeks and from Christianity, often linked happiness with morality, which was concerned with the performance in a certain kind of role in a certain kind of social life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Happiness remains a difficult term for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moral_philosophy" title="Moral philosophy">moral philosophy</a>. Throughout the history of moral philosophy, there has been an oscillation between attempts to define morality in terms of consequences leading to happiness and attempts to define morality in terms that have nothing to do with happiness at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Connections between happiness and morality have been studied in a variety of ways in psychology. Empirical research suggests that laypeople's judgments of a person's happiness in part depend on perceptions of that person's morality, suggesting that judgments of others' happiness involve moral evaluation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> A large body of research also suggests that engaging in prosocial behavior can increase happiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aknin2021_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aknin2021-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hui2020_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hui2020-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curry2018_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curry2018-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics">Ethics</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">Ethicists</a> have made arguments for how humans should behave, either individually or collectively, based on the resulting happiness of such behavior. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utilitarian" title="Utilitarian">Utilitarians</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">Jeremy Bentham</a>, advocated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greatest_happiness_principle" title="Greatest happiness principle">greatest happiness principle</a> as a guide for ethical behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aristotle">Aristotle</span></h3>
<p>Aristotle described <i><a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">eudaimonia</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a>: <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B5%E1%BD%90%CE%B4%CE%B1%CE%B9%CE%BC%CE%BF%CE%BD%CE%AF%CE%B1" title="wiktionary:Îµá½Î´Î±Î¹Î¼Î¿Î½Î¯Î±">Îµá½Î´Î±Î¹Î¼Î¿Î½Î¯Î±</a>) as the goal of human thought and action. Eudaimonia is often translated to mean happiness, but some scholars contend that "human flourishing" may be a more accurate translation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> Aristotle's use of the term in Nicomachiean Ethics extends beyond the general sense of happiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a></i>, written in 350 BCE, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> stated that happiness (also being well and doing well) is the only thing that humans desire for their own sake, unlike riches, honour, health or friendship. He observed that men sought riches, or honour, or health not only for their own sake but also in order to be happy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> For Aristotle the term <i><a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">eudaimonia</a></i>, which is translated as 'happiness' or 'flourishing' is an activity rather than an emotion or a state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> Eudaimonia (Greek: Îµá½Î´Î±Î¹Î¼Î¿Î½Î¯Î±) is a classical Greek word consists of the word "eu" ("good" or "well-being") and "daimÅn" ("spirit" or "minor deity", used by extension to mean one's lot or fortune). Thus understood, the happy life is the good life, that is, a life in which a person fulfills human nature in an excellent way. Specifically, Aristotle argued that the good life is the life of excellent rational activity. He arrived at this claim with the "Function Argument". Basically, if it is right, every living thing has a function, that which it uniquely does. For Aristotle human function is to reason, since it is that alone which humans uniquely do. And performing one's function well, or excellently, is good. According to Aristotle, the life of excellent rational activity is the happy life. Aristotle argued a second-best life for those incapable of excellent rational activity was the life of moral virtue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> The key question Aristotle seeks to answer is "What is the ultimate purpose of human existence?" a lot of people are seeking pleasure, health, and a good reputation. It is true that those have a value, but none of them can occupy the place of the greatest good for which humanity aims. It may seem like all goods are a means to obtain happiness, but Aristotle said that happiness is always an end in itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nietzsche">Nietzsche</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a> critiqued the English Utilitarians' focus on attaining the greatest happiness, stating that "Man does not strive for happiness, only the Englishman does".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> Nietzsche meant that making happiness one's ultimate <i>goal</i> and the aim of one's existence, in his words "makes one contemptible." Nietzsche instead yearned for a culture that would set higher, more difficult goals than "mere happiness." He introduced the quasi-dystopic figure of the "last man" as a kind of <a href="/wiki/Thought_experiment" title="Thought experiment">thought experiment</a> against the utilitarians and happiness-seekers. these small, "last men" who seek after only their own pleasure and health, avoiding all danger, exertion, difficulty, challenge, struggle are meant to seem contemptible to Nietzsche's reader. Nietzsche instead wants us to consider the value of what is difficult, what can only be earned through struggle, difficulty, pain and thus to come to see the affirmative value suffering and <i>unhappiness</i> truly play in creating everything of great worth in life, including all the highest achievements of human culture, not least of all philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_in_focus_over_time">Changes in focus over time</span></h3>
<p>In 2004 <a href="/wiki/Darrin_McMahon" title="Darrin McMahon">Darrin McMahon</a> said that over time the emphasis shifted from the happiness of virtue to the virtue of happiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes_and_achievement_methods">Causes and achievement methods</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Well-being_contributing_factors" title="Well-being contributing factors">Well-being contributing factors</a></div>
<p>Theories on how to achieve happiness include "encountering unexpected positive events",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> "seeing a significant other",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> and "basking in the acceptance and praise of others".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup>
Some others believe that happiness is not solely derived from external, momentary pleasures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Selig_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Selig-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research on positive psychology, well-being, eudaimonia and happiness, and the theories of Diener, Ryff, Keyes, and Seligmann covers a broad range of levels and topics, including "the biological, personal, relational, institutional, cultural, and global dimensions of life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeligmanCsikszentmihalyi2000_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeligmanCsikszentmihalyi2000-77">[76]</a></sup> The psychiatrist <a href="/wiki/George_Eman_Vaillant" title="George Eman Vaillant">George Vaillant</a> and the director of longitudinal <a href="/wiki/Grant_Study" title="Grant Study">Study of Adult Development</a> at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_J._Waldinger" title="Robert J. Waldinger">Robert J. Waldinger</a> found that those who were happiest and healthier reported strong interpersonal relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> Research showed that adequate sleep contributes to well-being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> Good <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental health</a> and good relationships contribute more than income to happiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> In 2018, <a href="/wiki/Laurie_R._Santos" title="Laurie R. Santos">Laurie R. Santos</a> course titled "<i>Psychology and the Good Life"</i> became the most popular course in the history of <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> and was made available for free online to non-Yale students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some commentators focus on the difference between the hedonistic tradition of seeking pleasant and avoiding unpleasant experiences, and the eudaimonic tradition of living life in a full and deeply satisfying way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-82">[81]</a></sup> Kahneman has said that "âWhen you look at what people want for themselves, how they pursue their goals, they seem more driven by the search for satisfaction than the search for happiness.â<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-fulfilment_theories">Self-fulfilment theories</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sweet_Baby_Kisses_Family_Love.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2782" data-file-width="4133" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Sweet_Baby_Kisses_Family_Love.jpg/220px-Sweet_Baby_Kisses_Family_Love.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Sweet_Baby_Kisses_Family_Love.jpg/330px-Sweet_Baby_Kisses_Family_Love.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Sweet_Baby_Kisses_Family_Love.jpg/440px-Sweet_Baby_Kisses_Family_Love.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sweet_Baby_Kisses_Family_Love.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Woman kissing a baby on the cheek</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Maslow%27s_hierarchy_of_needs" title="Maslow's hierarchy of needs">Maslow's hierarchy of needs</a> is a pyramid depicting the levels of human needs, psychological, and physical. When a human being ascends the steps of the pyramid, <a href="/wiki/Self-actualization" title="Self-actualization">self-actualization</a> is reached.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> Beyond the routine of needs fulfillment, Maslow envisioned moments of extraordinary experience, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peak_experiences" title="Peak experiences">peak experiences</a>, profound moments of love, understanding, happiness, or rapture, during which a person feels more whole, alive, self-sufficient, and yet a part of the world. This is similar to the <a href="/wiki/Flow_(psychology)" title="Flow (psychology)">flow</a> concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mih%C3%A1ly_Cs%C3%ADkszentmih%C3%A1lyi" title="MihÃ¡ly CsÃ­kszentmihÃ¡lyi">MihÃ¡ly CsÃ­kszentmihÃ¡lyi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> The concept of flow is the idea that after our basic needs are met we can achieve greater happiness by altering our consciousness by becoming so engaged in a task that we lose our sense of time. Our intense focus causes us to forget any other issues, which in return promotes positive emotions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Erich_Fromm" title="Erich Fromm">Erich Fromm</a> said <i>"Happiness is the indication that man has found the answer to the problem of human existence: the productive realization of his potentialities and thus, simultaneously, being one with the world and preserving the integrity of his self. In spending his energy productively he increases his powers, he âburns without being consumed.""</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Girl_of_Vietnam.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="631" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Girl_of_Vietnam.jpg/220px-Girl_of_Vietnam.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Girl_of_Vietnam.jpg/330px-Girl_of_Vietnam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Girl_of_Vietnam.jpg/440px-Girl_of_Vietnam.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Girl_of_Vietnam.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Smiling woman from Vietnam</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Self-determination_theory" title="Self-determination theory">Self-determination theory</a> relates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intrinsic_motivation" title="Intrinsic motivation">intrinsic motivation</a> to three needs: <a href="/wiki/Competence_(human_resources)" title="Competence (human resources)">competence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Autonomy" title="Autonomy">autonomy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Social_relation" title="Social relation">relatedness</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ronald_Inglehart" title="Ronald Inglehart">Ronald Inglehart</a> has traced cross-national differences in the level of happiness based on data from the <a href="/wiki/World_Values_Survey" title="World Values Survey">World Values Survey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inglehart2008_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Inglehart2008-88">[87]</a></sup> He finds that the extent to which a society allows free choice has a major impact on happiness. When <a href="/wiki/Basic_needs" title="Basic needs">basic needs</a> are satisfied, the degree of happiness depends on economic and cultural factors that enable free choice in how people live their lives. Happiness also depends on religion in countries where free choice is constrained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The idea of <a href="/wiki/Hedonism" title="Hedonism">motivational hedonism</a> is the theory that pleasure is the aim for human life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> Â Due to <a href="/wiki/Impact_bias" title="Impact bias">impact bias</a>, people are poor predictors of their future emotions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud#Happiness" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a> said that all humans strive after happiness, but that the possibilities of achieving it are restricted because we "are so made that we can derive intense enjoyment only from a contrast and very little from the <a href="/wiki/Status_quo" title="Status quo">state of things</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Positive_psychology">Positive psychology</span></h3>
<p>Since 2000 the field of <a href="/wiki/Positive_psychology" title="Positive psychology">positive psychology</a> has expanded drastically in terms of scientific publications, and has produced many different views on causes of happiness, and on factors that correlate with happiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYSciHap_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYSciHap-93">[92]</a></sup> Numerous short-term self-help interventions have been developed and demonstrated to improve happiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indirect_approaches">Indirect approaches</span></h3>
<p>Various writers, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camus" title="Camus">Camus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eckhart_Tolle" title="Eckhart Tolle">Tolle</a>, have written that the act of searching or seeking for happiness is incompatible with being happy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> believed that for the great majority of people happiness is best achieved en passant, rather than striving for it directly. This meant no self-consciousness, scrutiny, self-interrogation, dwelling on, thinking about,
imagining or questioning on one's happiness. Then, if otherwise fortunately circumstanced, one would "inhale happiness with the air you breathe."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/William_Inge_(priest)" title="William Inge (priest)">William Inge</a> said that "on the whole, the happiest people seem to be those who have no particular cause for being happy except the fact that they are so."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Orison_Swett_Marden" title="Orison Swett Marden">Orison Swett Marden</a> said that "some people are born happy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cognitive_Behavioral_Therapy">Cognitive Behavioral Therapy</span></h3>
<p>Cognitive Behavioral Therapy is a popular therapeutic method used to change habits by merely changing thoughts. It focuses on emotional regulation and utilizes a lot of positive psychology practices. It is often used for people with depression or anxiety, and works towards how to lead a happier life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Positive">Positive</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Negative">Negative</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/June_Gruber" title="June Gruber">June Gruber</a> argued that happiness may have negative effects. It may trigger a person to be more sensitive, more gullible, less successful, and more likely to undertake high risk behaviours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> She also conducted studies suggesting that seeking happiness can have negative effects, such as failure to meet over-high expectations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gruberpeplab.com_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-gruberpeplab.com-105">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Can_Seeking_Happiness_Make_People_H_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Can_Seeking_Happiness_Make_People_H-107">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iris_Mauss" title="Iris Mauss">Iris Mauss</a> has shown that the more people strive for happiness, the more likely they will set up too high of standards and feel disappointed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-108">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> The reason happiness can lead to negative effects is because it can lead to individualities self-defeating. Â A lot of times, the more people try to find happiness, the more disappointed they may feel. One study shows that women who value happiness more, tend to be less happy in low stress situations. Woman who also value high happiness tend to react less positively to happy emotions. This shows that with happiness those who make it their identity tend to be less happy than they expect to be when happy situations present themselves. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> A 2012 study found that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychological_well-being" title="Psychological well-being">psychological well-being</a> was higher for people who experienced both positive and negative emotions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_110527-N-OA833-014_Newly_commissioned_Navy_and_Marine_Corps_officers_toss_their_hats_during_the_U.S._Naval_Academy_Class_of_2011_graduation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2206" data-file-width="3120" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/US_Navy_110527-N-OA833-014_Newly_commissioned_Navy_and_Marine_Corps_officers_toss_their_hats_during_the_U.S._Naval_Academy_Class_of_2011_graduation.jpg/220px-US_Navy_110527-N-OA833-014_Newly_commissioned_Navy_and_Marine_Corps_officers_toss_their_hats_during_the_U.S._Naval_Academy_Class_of_2011_graduation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/US_Navy_110527-N-OA833-014_Newly_commissioned_Navy_and_Marine_Corps_officers_toss_their_hats_during_the_U.S._Naval_Academy_Class_of_2011_graduation.jpg/330px-US_Navy_110527-N-OA833-014_Newly_commissioned_Navy_and_Marine_Corps_officers_toss_their_hats_during_the_U.S._Naval_Academy_Class_of_2011_graduation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/US_Navy_110527-N-OA833-014_Newly_commissioned_Navy_and_Marine_Corps_officers_toss_their_hats_during_the_U.S._Naval_Academy_Class_of_2011_graduation.jpg/440px-US_Navy_110527-N-OA833-014_Newly_commissioned_Navy_and_Marine_Corps_officers_toss_their_hats_during_the_U.S._Naval_Academy_Class_of_2011_graduation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_110527-N-OA833-014_Newly_commissioned_Navy_and_Marine_Corps_officers_toss_their_hats_during_the_U.S._Naval_Academy_Class_of_2011_graduation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Newly commissioned officers celebrate their new positions by throwing their midshipmen covers into the air as part of the U.S. Naval Academy class of 2011 graduation and commissioning ceremony.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Happiness_economics" title="Happiness economics">Happiness economics</a></div>
<p>In politics, <i>happiness</i> as a guiding ideal is expressed in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">United States Declaration of Independence</a> of 1776, written by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, as the universal right to "the pursuit of happiness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> This seems to suggest a subjective interpretation but one that goes beyond emotions alone. It has to be kept in mind that the word <i>happiness</i> meant "prosperity, thriving, wellbeing" in the 18th century and not the same thing as it does today. In fact, <i>happiness</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>On average, richer nations tend to be happier than poorer nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frey2001_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frey2001-116">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cato2007_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cato2007-117">[116]</a></sup> This effect seems to diminish with wealth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frey2001_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frey2001-116">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cato2007_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cato2007-117">[116]</a></sup> because the dependency is not linear but logarithmic, i.e., the same percentage increase in the GNP produces the same increase in happiness for wealthy countries as for poor countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wealth_and_happiness_revisited_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-wealth_and_happiness_revisited-118">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-globe_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-globe-121">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Empirical research in the field of <a href="/wiki/Happiness_economics" title="Happiness economics">happiness economics</a>, such as that by political scientist <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Radcliff" title="Benjamin Radcliff">Benjamin Radcliff</a>, suggests that in democratic countries life satisfaction is strongly and positively related to the social democratic model of a generous <a href="/wiki/Social_safety_net" title="Social safety net">social safety net</a>, pro-worker labor market regulations, and strong labor unions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Synthesis_of_published_material" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag may be based upon an improper synthesis of sources. (August 2016)">improper synthesis?</span></a></i>]</sup> Similarly, there is evidence that public policies which reduce poverty and support a strong middle class, such as a higher minimum wage, strongly affect average levels of well-being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">Libertarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Think_tank" title="Think tank">think tank</a> <a href="/wiki/Cato_Institute" title="Cato Institute">Cato Institute</a> has said that happiness is strongly correlated with <a href="/wiki/Economic_freedom" title="Economic freedom">economic freedom</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cato.org_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-cato.org-125">[124]</a></sup> preferably within the context of a western mixed economy, with free press and a democracy. 
</p><p>Studies have found that East European countries before the fall of Communism were less happy than Western ones, and even less happy than other equally poor countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-sci-126">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_values">Cultural values</span></h3>
<p>Personal happiness can be affected by <a href="/wiki/Culture_and_positive_psychology" title="Culture and positive psychology">cultural factors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> Hedonism appears to be more strongly related to happiness in more individualistic cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>One theory is that higher SWB in richer countries is related to their more individualistic cultures. Individualistic cultures may satisfy intrinsic motivations to a higher degree that collectivistic cultures, and fulfilling intrinsic motivations, as opposed to extrinsic motivations, may relate to greater levels of happiness, leading to more happiness in individualistic cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cultural views on happiness have changed over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> For instance Western concern about childhood being a time of happiness has occurred only since the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> Not all cultures seek to maximize happiness,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-134">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeanne_Tsai_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jeanne_Tsai-135">[nb 2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-136">[nb 3]</a></sup> and some cultures are averse to happiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-137">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[135]</a></sup> It has been found in Western cultures that individual happiness is the most important. Some other cultures have opposite views and tend to be aversive to the idea of individual happiness. For example, people living in Eastern Asian cultures focus more on the need for happiness within relationships with others and even find personal happiness to be harmful to fulfilling happy social relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-137">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-134">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeanne_Tsai_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jeanne_Tsai-135">[nb 2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-136">[nb 3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Religious_studies" title="Religious studies">Religious studies</a></div>
<p>People in countries with high cultural religiosity tend to relate their life satisfaction less to their emotional experiences than people in more secular countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Buddhism">Buddhism</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Very_happy_Tibetan_Buddhist_Monk.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2800" data-file-width="1861" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Very_happy_Tibetan_Buddhist_Monk.jpg/170px-Very_happy_Tibetan_Buddhist_Monk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Very_happy_Tibetan_Buddhist_Monk.jpg/255px-Very_happy_Tibetan_Buddhist_Monk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Very_happy_Tibetan_Buddhist_Monk.jpg/340px-Very_happy_Tibetan_Buddhist_Monk.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Very_happy_Tibetan_Buddhist_Monk.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tibetan Buddhist monk</div></div></div>
<p>Happiness forms a central theme of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist teachings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[138]</a></sup> For ultimate freedom from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dukkha" title="Dukkha">suffering</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Noble_Eightfold_Path" title="Noble Eightfold Path">Noble Eightfold Path</a> leads its practitioner to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nirvana_(concept)" title="Nirvana (concept)">Nirvana</a>, a state of everlasting peace. Ultimate happiness is only achieved by overcoming <a href="/wiki/Ta%E1%B9%87h%C4%81" title="Taá¹hÄ">craving</a> in all forms. More mundane forms of happiness, such as acquiring wealth and maintaining good friendships, are also recognized as worthy goals for <a href="/wiki/Householder_(Buddhism)" title="Householder (Buddhism)">lay people</a> (see <i><a href="/wiki/Sukha" title="Sukha">sukha</a></i>). Buddhism also encourages the generation of <a href="/wiki/Maitr%C4%AB" title="MaitrÄ«">loving kindness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karu%E1%B9%87%C4%81" title="Karuá¹Ä">compassion</a>, the desire for the happiness and welfare of all beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[140]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (October 2011)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (October 2011)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hinduism">Hinduism</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a>, the ultimate goal of life is happiness, in the sense that duality between <a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ätman (Hinduism)">Atman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a> is transcended and one realizes oneself to be the Self in all.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Patanjali" title="Patanjali">Patanjali</a>, author of the <a href="/wiki/Yoga_Sutras_of_Patanjali" title="Yoga Sutras of Patanjali">Yoga Sutras</a>, wrote quite exhaustively on the psychological and ontological roots of bliss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Confucianism">Confucianism</span></h4>
<p>The Chinese Confucian thinker <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a>, who had sought to give advice to ruthless political leaders during China's Warring States period, was convinced that the mind played a mediating role between the "lesser self" (the physiological self) and the "greater self" (the moral self), and that getting the priorities right between these two would lead to sage-hood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[142]</a></sup> He argued that if one did not feel satisfaction or pleasure in nourishing one's "vital force" with "righteous deeds", then that force would shrivel up (Mencius, 6A:15 2A:2). More specifically, he mentions the experience of intoxicating joy if one celebrates the practice of the great virtues, especially through music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Judaism">Judaism</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Happiness_in_Judaism" title="Happiness in Judaism">Happiness in Judaism</a></div>
<p>Happiness or <i><a href="/wiki/Simcha" title="Simcha">simcha</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="he">×©×××</span>) in Judaism is considered an important element in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avodat_Hashem" title="Avodat Hashem">service of God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[144]</a></sup> The biblical verse "worship The Lord with gladness; come before him with joyful songs," (<a class="external text" href="https://www.biblica.com/bible/?osis=niv:Psalms%20100:2" rel="nofollow">Psalm 100:2</a>) stresses joy in the service of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[145]</a></sup> A popular teaching by Rabbi <a href="/wiki/Nachman_of_Breslov" title="Nachman of Breslov">Nachman of Breslov</a>, a 19th-century Chassidic Rabbi, is "<i>Mitzvah Gedolah Le'hiyot Besimcha Tamid</i>," it is a great <a href="/wiki/Mitzvah" title="Mitzvah">mitzvah</a> (commandment) to always be in a state of happiness. When a person is happy they are much more capable of serving God and going about their daily activities than when <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depressed</a> or upset.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[146]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (August 2016)">self-published source?</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Beatitudes" title="The Beatitudes">The Beatitudes</a></div>
<p>The primary meaning of "happiness" in various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_languages" title="European languages">European languages</a> involves <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Good_fortune" title="Good fortune">good fortune</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blessing" title="Blessing">blessing</a>, or a similar happening. The meaning in Greek philosophy refers primarily to ethics.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, the ultimate end of human existence consists in felicity, Latin equivalent to the Greek <i><a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">eudaimonia</a></i> ("blessed happiness"), described by the 13th-century philosopher-theologian <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beatific_Vision" title="Beatific Vision">Beatific Vision</a> of God's essence in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Next_life" title="Next life">next life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, man's last end is happiness: "all men agree in desiring the last end, which is happiness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[148]</a></sup> Aquinas agreed with <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> that happiness cannot be reached solely through reasoning about consequences of acts, but also requires a pursuit of good causes for acts, such as habits according to <a href="/wiki/Virtue" title="Virtue">virtue</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Aquinas, happiness consists in an "operation of the speculative <a href="/wiki/Intellect" title="Intellect">intellect</a>": "Consequently happiness consists principally in such an operation, viz. in the contemplation of Divine things." And, "the last end cannot consist in the active life, which pertains to the practical intellect." So: "Therefore the last and perfect happiness, which we await in the life to come, consists entirely in contemplation. But imperfect happiness, such as can be had here, consists first and principally in contemplation, but secondarily, in an operation of the practical intellect directing human actions and passions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>Human complexities, like reason and cognition, can produce well-being or happiness, but such form is limited and transitory. In temporal life, the contemplation of God, the infinitely Beautiful, is the supreme delight of the will. <i>Beatitudo</i>, or perfect happiness, as complete well-being, is to be attained not in this life, but the next.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a> (1058â1111), the  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> thinker, wrote that "<a href="/wiki/The_Alchemy_of_Happiness" title="The Alchemy of Happiness">The Alchemy of Happiness</a>", is a manual of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_instruction" title="Religious instruction">religious instruction</a> that is used throughout the Muslim world and widely practiced today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics_and_heritability">Genetics and heritability</span></h2>
<p>As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Happiness&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, no evidence of happiness causing improved physical health has been found; the topic is being researched at the Lee Kum Sheung Center for Health and Happiness at the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_T.H._Chan_School_of_Public_Health" title="Harvard T.H. Chan School of Public Health">Harvard T.H. Chan School of Public Health</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harvardmaggudrais_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-harvardmaggudrais-156">[153]</a></sup>
A positive relationship has been suggested between the volume of the brain's gray matter in the right <a href="/wiki/Precuneus" title="Precuneus">precuneus</a> area and one's subjective happiness score.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sonja_Lyubomirsky" title="Sonja Lyubomirsky">Sonja Lyubomirsky</a> has estimated that 50 percent of a given human's happiness level could be genetically determined, 10 percent is affected by life circumstances and situation, and a remaining 40 percent of happiness is subject to self-control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>When discussing genetics and their effects on individuals it is important to first understand that genetics do not predict behavior. It is possible for genes to increase the likelihood of individuals being happier compared to others, but they do not 100 percent predict behavior.
</p><p>At this point in scientific research, it has been hard to find a lot of evidence to support this idea that happiness is affected in some way by genetics. In a 2016 study, Michael Minkov and Michael Harris Bond found that a gene by the name of SLC6A4 was not a good predictor of happiness level in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the other hand, there have been many studies that have found genetics to be a key part in predicting and understanding happiness in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[158]</a></sup> In a review article discussing many studies on genetics and happiness, they discussed the common findings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[159]</a></sup> The author found an important factor that has affected scientist findings this being how happiness is measured. For example, in certain studies when subjective wellbeing is measured as a trait heredity is found to be higher, about 70 to 90 percent. In another study, 11,500 unrelated genotypes were studied, and the conclusion was the heritability was only 12 to 18 percent. Overall, this article found the common percent of heredity was about 20 to 50 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[160]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dan Haybron (<a class="external free" href="https://www.slu.edu/colleges/AS/philos/site/people/faculty/Haybron/" rel="nofollow">https://www.slu.edu/colleges/AS/philos/site/people/faculty/Haybron/</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20190830115908/https://www.slu.edu/colleges/AS/philos/site/people/faculty/Haybron/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 30 August 2019 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, <a class="external free" href="http://www.happinessandwellbeing.org/project-team/" rel="nofollow">http://www.happinessandwellbeing.org/project-team/</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20181012094438/http://www.happinessandwellbeing.org/project-team/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 12 October 2018 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>); "<i>I would suggest that when we talk about happiness, we are actually referring, much of the time, to a complex emotional phenomenon. Call it emotional well-being. Happiness as emotional well-being concerns your emotions and moods, more broadly your emotional condition as a whole. To be happy is to inhabit a favorable emotional state.... On this view, we can think of happiness, loosely, as the opposite of anxiety and depression. Being in good spirits, quick to laugh and slow to anger, at peace and untroubled, confident and comfortable in your own skin, engaged, energetic and full of life.</i>" <a class="external free" href="https://opinionator.blogs.nytimes.com/2014/04/13/happiness-and-its-discontents/" rel="nofollow">https://opinionator.blogs.nytimes.com/2014/04/13/happiness-and-its-discontents/</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20181012094415/https://opinionator.blogs.nytimes.com/2014/04/13/happiness-and-its-discontents/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 12 October 2018 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> Haybron has also used the term thymic, by which he means 'overall mood state' in this context; <a class="external free" href="https://philpapers.org/rec/HAYHAE" rel="nofollow">https://philpapers.org/rec/HAYHAE</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20181018003005/https://philpapers.org/rec/HAYHAE" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 18 October 2018 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>  Xavier Landes &lt;<a class="external free" href="https://www.sseriga.edu/landes-xavier" rel="nofollow">https://www.sseriga.edu/landes-xavier</a>&gt; <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20190830115908/https://www.sseriga.edu/landes-xavier" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 30 August 2019 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>&gt; has described a similar concept of <a href="/wiki/Mood_(psychology)" title="Mood (psychology)">mood</a>. <a class="external free" href="https://www.satori.lv/article/kas-ir-laime" rel="nofollow">https://www.satori.lv/article/kas-ir-laime</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20190513122332/https://www.satori.lv/article/kas-ir-laime" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 13 May 2019 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"<i>People don't want to be happy the way I've defined the term â what I experience here and now. In my view, it's much more important for them to be satisfied, to experience life satisfaction, from the perspective of 'What I remember,' of the story they tell about their lives.</i>"<a class="external free" href="https://www.haaretz.com/israel-news/.premium.MAGAZINE-why-nobel-prize-winner-daniel-kahneman-gave-up-on-happiness-1.6528513" rel="nofollow">https://www.haaretz.com/israel-news/.premium.MAGAZINE-why-nobel-prize-winner-daniel-kahneman-gave-up-on-happiness-1.6528513</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20181008135016/https://www.haaretz.com/israel-news/.premium.MAGAZINE-why-nobel-prize-winner-daniel-kahneman-gave-up-on-happiness-1.6528513" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 8 October 2018 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></span>

Outskirts Press. pp.Â 6â10. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>Â <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1478722595" title="Special:BookSources/978-1478722595"><bdi>978-1478722595</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Facts+of+Life%3A+ten+issues+of+contentment&amp;rft.pages=6-10&amp;rft.pub=Outskirts+Press&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft.isbn=978-1478722595&amp;rft.aulast=Graham&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael+C.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHappiness"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external free" href="https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/happiness/" rel="nofollow">https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/happiness/</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20180611093213/https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/happiness/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 11 June 2018 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> 2011, "'Happiness' is often used, in ordinary life, to refer to a short-lived state of a person, frequently a feeling of contentment: 'You look happy today'; 'I'm very happy for you'. Philosophically, its scope is more often wider, encompassing a whole life. And in philosophy it is possible to speak of the happiness of a person's life, or of their happy life, even if that person was in fact usually pretty miserable. The point is that some good things in their life made it a happy one, even though they lacked contentment. But this usage is uncommon, and may cause confusion.' <a class="external free" href="https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/well-being/" rel="nofollow">https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/well-being/</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20181025225319/https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/well-being/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 25 October 2018 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> 2017</span>
</li>

In my view, it's much more important for them to be satisfied, to experience life satisfaction, from the perspective of 'What I remember,' of the story they tell about their lives.</i>"<a class="external free" href="https://www.haaretz.com/israel-news/.premium.MAGAZINE-why-nobel-prize-winner-daniel-kahneman-gave-up-on-happiness-1.6528513" rel="nofollow">https://www.haaretz.com/israel-news/.premium.MAGAZINE-why-nobel-prize-winner-daniel-kahneman-gave-up-on-happiness-1.6528513</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20181008135016/https://www.haaretz.com/israel-news/.premium.MAGAZINE-why-nobel-prize-winner-daniel-kahneman-gave-up-on-happiness-1.6528513" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 2018-10-08 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></span>

<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"<i>Surveying large numbers of Americans in one case, and what is claimed to be the first globally representative sample of humanity in the other, these studies found that income does indeed correlate substantially (.44 in the global sample), at all levels, with life satisfactionÂ â strictly speaking, a "life evaluation" measure that asks respondents to rate their lives without saying whether they are satisfied. Yet the correlation of household income with the affect measures is far weaker: globally, .17 for positive affect, -.09 for negative affect; and in the United States, essentially zero above $75,000 (though quite strong at low income levels). If the results hold up, the upshot appears to be that income is pretty strongly related to life satisfaction, but weakly related to emotional well-being, at least above a certain threshold.</i>" Section 3.3, Happiness, Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy, <a class="external free" href="https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/happiness/#HedVerEmoSta" rel="nofollow">https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/happiness/#HedVerEmoSta</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20180611093213/https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/happiness/#HedVerEmoSta" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 2018-06-11 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></span>

<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFInc2015">Inc, Gallup (24 April 2015). <a class="external text" href="https://news.gallup.com/opinion/gallup/182843/happiest-people-world-swiss-latin-americans.aspx" rel="nofollow">"Who Are the Happiest People in the World? The Swiss or Latin Americans?"</a>. <i>Gallup.com</i>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Gallup.com&amp;rft.atitle=Who+Are+the+Happiest+People+in+the+World%3F+The+Swiss+or+Latin+Americans%3F&amp;rft.date=2015-04-24&amp;rft.aulast=Inc&amp;rft.aufirst=Gallup&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fnews.gallup.com%2Fopinion%2Fgallup%2F182843%2Fhappiest-people-world-swiss-latin-americans.aspx&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHappiness"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation report cs1" id="CITEREFHelliwellYang2012"><a href="/wiki/John_Helliwell" title="John Helliwell">Helliwell, John</a>; Yang, Shun (2012). <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160718204801/http://worldhappiness.report/ed/2012/" rel="nofollow">World Happiness Report 2012</a> (Report). p.Â 11. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://worldhappiness.report/ed/2012/" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 18 July 2016. <q>How does happiness come into this classification? For better or worse, it enters in three ways. It is sometimes used as a current emotional report â "How happy are you now?," sometimes as a remembered emotion, as in "How happy were you yesterday?," and very often as a form of life evaluation, as in "How happy are you with your life as a whole these days?" People answer these three types of happiness question differently, so it is important to keep track of what is being asked. The good news is that the answers differ in ways that suggest that people understand what they are being asked, and answer appropriately</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=report&amp;rft.btitle=World+Happiness+Report+2012&amp;rft.pages=11&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.aulast=Helliwell&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rft.au=Yang%2C+Shun&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fworldhappiness.report%2Fed%2F2012%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHappiness"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation news cs1" id="CITEREFChernoff2002">Chernoff, Naina N. (6 May 2002). <a class="external text" href="https://www.psychologicalscience.org/observer/memory-vs-experience-happiness-is-relative#:~:text=The%20contrast%20between%20remembered%20and,gets%20to%20keep%20the%20memories" rel="nofollow">"Memory Vs. Experience: Happiness is Relative"</a>. <i>Observer</i>. Association for Psychological Science<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">10 November</span> 2021</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Observer&amp;rft.atitle=Memory+Vs.+Experience%3A+Happiness+is+Relative&amp;rft.date=2002-05-06&amp;rft.aulast=Chernoff&amp;rft.aufirst=Naina+N.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.psychologicalscience.org%2Fobserver%2Fmemory-vs-experience-happiness-is-relative%23%3A~%3Atext%3DThe%2520contrast%2520between%2520remembered%2520and%2Cgets%2520to%2520keep%2520the%2520memories&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHappiness"></span></span>
